<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Acid dissociation constant</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>

<p>An <b>acid dissociation constant</b>, <i>K</i><sub>a</sub>, (also known as <b>acidity constant</b>, or <b>acid-ionization constant</b>) is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantitative_property" title="Quantitative property">quantitative</a> measure of the <a href="/wiki/Acid_strength" title="Acid strength">strength</a> of an <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acid</a> in <a href="/wiki/Solution" title="Solution">solution</a>. It is the <a href="/wiki/Equilibrium_constant" title="Equilibrium constant">equilibrium constant</a> for a chemical reaction known as <a href="/wiki/Dissociation_(chemistry)" title="Dissociation (chemistry)">dissociation</a> in the context of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acid-base_reaction" title="Acid-base reaction">acid–base reactions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[note 1]</a></sup> In aqueous solution, the equilibrium of acid dissociation can be written symbolically as:
</p>

</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {HA + H2O &lt;=&gt; A^- + H3O^+}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/974f0d221871c1057f724f22e2425259a1d9b9c7" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:27.791ex; height:3.343ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>where HA is a generic <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acid</a> that dissociates into A<sup>−</sup>, known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conjugate_base" title="Conjugate base">conjugate base</a> of the acid and a <a href="/wiki/Hydron_(chemistry)" title="Hydron (chemistry)">hydrogen ion</a> which combines with a water molecule to make a <a href="/wiki/Hydronium" title="Hydronium">hydronium</a> ion. In the example shown in the figure, HA represents <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a>, and A<sup>−</sup> represents the <a href="/wiki/Acetate" title="Acetate">acetate</a> ion, the conjugate base.
</p><p>The chemical species HA, H<sub>2</sub>O, A<sup>−</sup> and H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup> are said to be in <a href="/wiki/Chemical_equilibrium" title="Chemical equilibrium">equilibrium</a> when their concentrations (written below in square brackets) do not change with the passing of time. The dissociation constant is usually defined for a simplified reaction equation in which the solvent H<sub>2</sub>O is ignored:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {HA &lt;=&gt; A^- + H+}};K_{{\ce {a}}}={\frac {[{\ce {A^-}}][{\ce {H+}}]}{{\ce {[HA]}}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {HA &lt;=&gt; A^- + H+}};K_{{\ce {a}}}={\frac {[{\ce {A^-}}][{\ce {H+}}]}{{\ce {[HA]}}}}}</annotation>

</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {HA &lt;=&gt; A^- + H+}};K_{{\ce {a}}}={\frac {[{\ce {A^-}}][{\ce {H+}}]}{{\ce {[HA]}}}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/eb2c0c42b1bf09a7c5e377407b9049287046f7e4" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:34.573ex; height:6.676ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>A simple reason often given for ignoring the concentration of water here is that it remains essentially constant when the ionization occurs in dilute aqueous solution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[1]</a></sup> More correctly, the equilibrium constant should be written in terms of <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_activity" title="Thermodynamic activity">thermodynamic activities</a> instead of concentrations. While the activity of each solute is approximated by its concentration, the activity of solvent water using pure water as a <a href="/wiki/Standard_state" title="Standard state">standard state</a> is approximated as one so that it can be ignored in the equation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>This is the definition in common usage. For many practical purposes it is more convenient to discuss the logarithmic constant, p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathrm {p} K_{\mathrm {a} }=-\log _{10}\left(K_{\mathrm {a} }\right)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathrm {p} K_{\mathrm {a} }=-\log _{10}\left(K_{\mathrm {a} }\right)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \mathrm {p} K_{\mathrm {a} }=-\log _{10}\left(K_{\mathrm {a} }\right)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5c53bff8acb05c000091f6c71262826b73728fa9" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:19.298ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[note 2]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>The more positive the value of p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub>, the smaller the extent of dissociation at any given pH (see <a href="/wiki/Henderson%E2%80%93Hasselbalch_equation" title="Henderson–Hasselbalch equation">Henderson–Hasselbalch equation</a>)—that is, the weaker the acid. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_acid" title="Weak acid">weak acid</a> has a p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> value in the approximate range −2 to 12 in water. Acids with a p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> value of less than about −2 are said to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strong_acids" title="Strong acids">strong acids</a>; the dissociation of a strong acid is effectively complete such that concentration of the undissociated acid is too small to be measured. p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values for strong acids can, however, be estimated by theoretical means.
</p><p>The definition can be extended to non-aqueous solvents, such as <a href="/wiki/Acetonitrile" title="Acetonitrile">acetonitrile</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dimethylsulfoxide" title="Dimethylsulfoxide">dimethylsulfoxide</a>. Denoting a solvent molecule by <b>S</b>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theoretical_background">Theoretical background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Acid_dissociation_constant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Theoretical background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The acid dissociation constant for an acid is a direct consequence of the underlying <a href="/wiki/Chemical_thermodynamics" title="Chemical thermodynamics">thermodynamics</a> of the dissociation reaction; the p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> value is directly proportional to the standard <a href="/wiki/Gibbs_free_energy" title="Gibbs free energy">Gibbs free energy</a> change for the reaction. The value of the p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> changes with temperature and can be understood qualitatively based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Le_Ch%C3%A2telier%27s_principle" title="Le Châtelier's principle">Le Châtelier's principle</a>: when the reaction is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endothermic" title="Endothermic">endothermic</a>, <i>K</i><sub>a</sub> increases and p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> decreases with increasing temperature; the opposite is true for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exothermic" title="Exothermic">exothermic</a> reactions.
</p><p>The value of p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> also depends on molecular structure of the acid in many ways. For example, <a href="/wiki/Linus_Pauling" title="Linus Pauling">Pauling</a> proposed two rules: one for successive p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> of polyprotic acids (see <a href="/wiki/Acid_dissociation_constant#Polyprotic_acids" title="Acid dissociation constant">Polyprotic acids</a> below), and one to estimate the p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> of oxyacids based on the number of =O and −OH groups (see <a href="/wiki/Acid_dissociation_constant#Factors_that_affect_pKa_values" title="Acid dissociation constant">Factors that affect p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values</a> below). Other structural factors that influence the magnitude of the acid dissociation constant include <a href="/wiki/Inductive_effect" title="Inductive effect">inductive effects</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesomeric_effect" title="Mesomeric effect">mesomeric effects</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bonding" title="Hydrogen bonding">hydrogen bonding</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hammett_equation" title="Hammett equation">Hammett type equations</a> have frequently been applied to the estimation of p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perrin_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perrin-5">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RMC_2013_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-RMC_2013-6">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The quantitative behaviour of acids and bases in solution can be understood only if their p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values are known. In particular, the <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> of a solution can be predicted when the analytical concentration and p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values of all acids and bases are known; conversely, it is possible to calculate the equilibrium concentration of the acids and bases in solution when the pH is known. These calculations find application in many different areas of chemistry, biology, medicine, and geology. For example, many compounds used for medication are weak acids or bases, and a knowledge of the p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values, together with the <a href="/wiki/Partition_coefficient" title="Partition coefficient">water–octanol partition coefficient</a>, can be used for estimating the extent to which the compound enters the blood stream. Acid dissociation constants are also essential in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aquatic_chemistry" title="Aquatic chemistry">aquatic chemistry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chemical_oceanography" title="Chemical oceanography">chemical oceanography</a>, where the acidity of water plays a fundamental role. In living organisms, <a href="/wiki/Acid%E2%80%93base_homeostasis" title="Acid–base homeostasis">acid–base homeostasis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Enzyme_kinetics" title="Enzyme kinetics">enzyme kinetics</a> are dependent on the p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values of the many acids and bases present in the cell and in the body. In chemistry, a knowledge of p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values is necessary for the preparation of <a href="/wiki/Buffer_solution" title="Buffer solution">buffer solutions</a> and is also a prerequisite for a quantitative understanding of the interaction between acids or bases and metal ions to form <a href="/wiki/Stability_constants_of_complexes" title="Stability constants of complexes">complexes</a>. Experimentally, p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values can be determined by potentiometric (pH) <a href="/wiki/Titration" title="Titration">titration</a>, but for values of p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> less than about 2 or more than about 11, <a href="/wiki/Spectrophotometry" title="Spectrophotometry">spectrophotometric</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_resonance" title="Nuclear magnetic resonance">NMR</a> measurements may be required due to practical difficulties with pH measurements.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Acid_dissociation_constant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acid-base_reaction#Arrhenius_definition" title="Acid-base reaction">Arrhenius</a>'s original definition, an acid is a substance that <a href="/wiki/Dissociation_(chemistry)" title="Dissociation (chemistry)">dissociates</a> in aqueous solution, releasing the hydrogen ion H<sup>+</sup> (a proton):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miessler_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miessler-7">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>HA ⇌ A<sup>−</sup> + H<sup>+</sup>.</dd></dl>
<p>The equilibrium constant for this dissociation reaction is known as a <a href="/wiki/Dissociation_constant" title="Dissociation constant">dissociation constant</a>. The liberated proton combines with a water molecule to give a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydronium_ion" title="Hydronium ion">hydronium (or oxonium) ion</a> H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup> (naked protons do not exist in solution), and so Arrhenius later proposed that the dissociation should be written as an <a href="/wiki/Acid%E2%80%93base_reaction" title="Acid–base reaction">acid–base reaction</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>HA + H<sub>2</sub>O ⇌ A<sup>−</sup> + H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup>.</dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Acetic-acid-dissociation-3D-balls.png"><img alt="Acetic acid, CH3COOH, is composed of a methyl group, CH3, bound chemically to a carboxylate group, COOH. The carboxylate group can lose a proton and donate it to a water molecule, H2O, leaving behind an acetate anion CH3COO− and creating a hydronium cation H3O. This is an equilibrium reaction, so the reverse process can also take place." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="513" data-file-width="2150" height="84" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Acetic-acid-dissociation-3D-balls.png/350px-Acetic-acid-dissociation-3D-balls.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Acetic-acid-dissociation-3D-balls.png/525px-Acetic-acid-dissociation-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Acetic-acid-dissociation-3D-balls.png/700px-Acetic-acid-dissociation-3D-balls.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Acetic-acid-dissociation-3D-balls.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">Acetic acid</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_acid" title="Weak acid">weak acid</a>, donates a proton (hydrogen ion, highlighted in green) to water in an equilibrium reaction to give the <a href="/wiki/Acetate" title="Acetate">acetate</a> ion and the <a href="/wiki/Hydronium" title="Hydronium">hydronium</a> ion. Red: oxygen, black: carbon, white: hydrogen.</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Br%C3%B8nsted%E2%80%93Lowry_acid-base_theory" title="Brønsted–Lowry acid-base theory">Brønsted and Lowry</a> generalised this further to a proton exchange reaction:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SA_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-SA-9">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>acid + base ⇌ conjugate base + conjugate acid.</dd></dl>
<p>The acid loses a proton, leaving a conjugate base; the proton is transferred to the base, creating a conjugate acid. For aqueous solutions of an acid HA, the base is water; the conjugate base is A<sup>−</sup> and the conjugate acid is the hydronium ion. The Brønsted–Lowry definition applies to other solvents, such as <a href="/wiki/Dimethyl_sulfoxide" title="Dimethyl sulfoxide">dimethyl sulfoxide</a>: the solvent S acts as a base, accepting a proton and forming the conjugate acid SH<sup>+</sup>.
</p>
<dl><dd>HA + S ⇌ A<sup>−</sup> + SH<sup>+</sup>.</dd></dl>
<p>In solution chemistry, it is common to use H<sup>+</sup> as an abbreviation for the solvated hydrogen ion, regardless of the solvent. In aqueous solution H<sup>+</sup> denotes a <a href="/wiki/Hydronium#Solvation" title="Hydronium">solvated hydronium ion</a> rather than a proton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Headrick_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Headrick-11">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smiechowski_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smiechowski-12">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>The designation of an acid or base as "conjugate" depends on the context. The conjugate acid BH<sup>+</sup> of a base B dissociates according to
</p>
<dl><dd>BH<sup>+</sup> + OH<sup>−</sup> ⇌ B + H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>which is the reverse of the equilibrium
</p>
<dl><dd>H<sub>2</sub>O (acid) + B (base) ⇌ OH<sup>−</sup> (conjugate base) + BH<sup>+</sup> (conjugate acid).</dd></dl>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxide_ion" title="Hydroxide ion">hydroxide ion</a> OH<sup>−</sup>, a well known base, is here acting as the conjugate base of the acid water. Acids and bases are thus regarded simply as donors and acceptors of protons respectively.
</p><p>A broader definition of acid dissociation includes <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a>, in which protons are produced by the splitting of water molecules. For example, <a href="/wiki/Boric_acid" title="Boric acid">boric acid</a> (B(OH)<sub>3</sub>) produces H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup> as if it were a proton donor,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldmine_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldmine-13">[11]</a></sup> but it has been confirmed by <a href="/wiki/Raman_spectroscopy" title="Raman spectroscopy">Raman spectroscopy</a> that this is due to the hydrolysis equilibrium:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>B(OH)<sub>3</sub> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O ⇌ B(OH)<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> + H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup>.</dd></dl>
<p>Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis#Hydrolysis_of_metal_aqua_ions" title="Hydrolysis">metal ion hydrolysis</a> causes ions such as <span style="white-space:nowrap;">[Al(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>3+</sup></span> to behave as weak acids:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burgess_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burgess-15">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>[Al(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>3+</sup> + H<sub>2</sub>O ⇌ [Al(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>5</sub>(OH)]<sup>2+</sup> + H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup>.</dd></dl>
<p>According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acid-base_reaction#Lewis_definition" title="Acid-base reaction">Lewis</a>'s original definition, an acid is a substance that accepts an <a href="/wiki/Electron_pair" title="Electron pair">electron pair</a> to form a <a href="/wiki/Coordinate_covalent_bond" title="Coordinate covalent bond">coordinate covalent bond</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Petrucci_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Petrucci-16">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Equilibrium_constant">Equilibrium constant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Acid_dissociation_constant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Equilibrium constant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>An acid dissociation constant is a particular example of an <a href="/wiki/Equilibrium_constant" title="Equilibrium constant">equilibrium constant</a>. For the specific equilibrium between a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monoprotic_acid" title="Monoprotic acid">monoprotic acid</a>, HA and its conjugate base A<sup>−</sup>, in water,
</p>
<dl><dd>HA + H<sub>2</sub>O ⇌ A<sup>−</sup> + H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup></dd></dl>
<p>the thermodynamic equilibrium constant, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle K^{\ominus }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle K^{\ominus }={\frac {\{{\ce {A^-}}\}\{{\ce {H3O+}}\}}{{\ce {\{HA\} \{H2O\}}}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>where {A} is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Activity_(chemistry)" title="Activity (chemistry)">activity</a> of the chemical species A, etc. <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle K^{\ominus }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

Activities of the products of dissociation are placed in the numerator, activities of the reactants are placed in the denominator. See <a href="/wiki/Activity_coefficient" title="Activity coefficient">activity coefficient</a> for a derivation of this expression.

<p>where [HA] represents the concentration of HA and <i>Γ</i> is a quotient of activity coefficients.
</p><p>To avoid the complications involved in using activities, dissociation constants are <a href="/wiki/Determination_of_equilibrium_constants" title="Determination of equilibrium constants">determined</a>, where possible, in a medium of high <a href="/wiki/Ionic_strength" title="Ionic strength">ionic strength</a>, that is, under conditions in which <i>Γ</i> can be assumed to be always constant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rr_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-rr-17">[15]</a></sup> For example, the medium might be a solution of 0.1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molar_(unit)" title="Molar (unit)">molar</a> (M) <a href="/wiki/Sodium_nitrate" title="Sodium nitrate">sodium nitrate</a> or 3 M <a href="/wiki/Potassium_perchlorate" title="Potassium perchlorate">potassium perchlorate</a>. Furthermore, in all but the most concentrated solutions it can be assumed that the concentration of water, [H<sub>2</sub>O], is constant, approximately 55 M. On dividing <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle K^{\ominus }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mi>K</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>⊖<!-- ⊖ --></mo>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle K^{\ominus }}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle K^{\ominus }}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1f45734886910651b2143da8310f2b6a094327a4" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:3.605ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> by the constant terms and writing [H<sup>+</sup>] for the concentration of the hydronium ion the expression
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {a} }=\mathrm {\frac {[A^{-}][H^{+}]}{[HA]}} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">[</mo>

<mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi>

</mrow>
</msup>
<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
<mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
<msup>
<mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>+</mo>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
</mrow>
<mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
<mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {a} }=\mathrm {\frac {[A^{-}][H^{+}]}{[HA]}} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {a} }=\mathrm {\frac {[A^{-}][H^{+}]}{[HA]}} }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4df1424aa532585fc7ad6dd660a596705ee31a2b" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:16.057ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>is obtained. This is the definition in common use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUPAC-NMR_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-IUPAC-NMR-18">[16]</a></sup> p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> is defined as −log<sub>10</sub>(<i>K</i><sub>a</sub>).
</p><p>Note, however, that all published dissociation constant values refer to the specific ionic medium used in their determination and that different values are obtained with different conditions, as shown for <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a> in the illustration above. When published constants refer to an ionic strength other than the one required for a particular application, they may be adjusted by means of specific ion theory (SIT) and other theories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Using the equation as shown <i>K</i><sub>a</sub> has <a href="/wiki/Dimensional_analysis" title="Dimensional analysis">dimensions</a> of concentration, but the exact definition uses chemical activities, which can be dimensionless. Therefore, <i>K</i><sub>a</sub>, as defined properly, is also dimensionless. But as defined here it is correct to quote a value with a dimension as, for example, "<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> = 300 M".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monoprotic_acids">Monoprotic acids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Acid_dissociation_constant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Monoprotic acids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Acid#Monoprotic_acids" title="Acid">Acid § Monoprotic acids</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Weak_acid_speciation.svg"><img alt="This figure plots the relative fractions of the protonated form A H of an acid to its deprotonated form, A minus, as the solution p H is varied about the value of the acid's p K A. When the p H equals the p K a, the amounts of the protonated and deprotonated forms are equal. When the p H is one unit higher than the p K A, the ratio of concentrations of protonated to deprotonated forms is 10 to 1. When the p H is two units higher that ratio is 100 to 1. Conversely, when the p H is one or two unit lower than the p K A, the ratio is 1 to ten or 1 to 100. The exact percentage of each form may be determined from the Henderson–Hasselbalch equation." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Weak_acid_speciation.svg/200px-Weak_acid_speciation.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Weak_acid_speciation.svg/300px-Weak_acid_speciation.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Weak_acid_speciation.svg/400px-Weak_acid_speciation.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Weak_acid_speciation.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Variation of the % formation of a monoprotic acid, AH, and its conjugate base, A<sup>−</sup>, with the difference between the pH and the p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> of the acid.</div></div></div>
<p>After rearranging the expression defining <i>K</i><sub>a</sub>, and putting <span class="nowrap">pH = −log<sub>10</sub>[H<sup>+</sup>]</span>, one obtains<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathrm {pH} =\mathrm {p} K_{\mathrm {a} }+\log \left(\mathrm {\frac {[A^{-}]}{[HA]}} \right)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">p</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">p</mi>
</mrow>
<msub>
<mi>K</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>log</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mrow>
<mo>(</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
<msup>
<mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
</mrow>
<mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
<mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>)</mo>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathrm {pH} =\mathrm {p} K_{\mathrm {a} }+\log \left(\mathrm {\frac {[A^{-}]}{[HA]}} \right)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \mathrm {pH} =\mathrm {p} K_{\mathrm {a} }+\log \left(\mathrm {\frac {[A^{-}]}{[HA]}} \right)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/247c29fdb6eff4332933c19dec43e6a60439c0d9" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:25.303ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>This is the <a href="/wiki/Henderson%E2%80%93Hasselbalch_equation" title="Henderson–Hasselbalch equation">Henderson–Hasselbalch equation</a>, from which the following conclusions can be drawn.
</p>
<ul><li>At half-neutralization the ratio <span class="nowrap"><span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">[A<sup>−</sup>]</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">[HA]</span></span> = 1</span>; since <span class="nowrap">log(1) = 0</span>, the pH at half-neutralization is numerically equal to p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub>. Conversely, when <span class="nowrap">pH = p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub></span>, the concentration of HA is equal to the concentration of A<sup>−</sup>.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Buffer_solution" title="Buffer solution">buffer region</a> extends over the approximate range p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> ± 2. Buffering is weak outside the range p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> ± 1. At pH ≤ p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> – 2 the substance is said to be fully protonated and at pH ≥ p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> + 2 it is fully dissociated (deprotonated).</li>
<li>If the pH is known, the ratio may be calculated. This ratio is independent of the analytical concentration of the acid.</li></ul>
<p>In water, measurable p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values range from about −2 for a strong acid to about 12 for a very weak acid (or strong base).
A <a href="/wiki/Buffer_solution" title="Buffer solution">buffer solution</a> of a desired pH can be prepared as a mixture of a weak acid and its conjugate base. In practice the mixture can be created by dissolving the acid in water, and adding the requisite amount of strong acid or base. When the p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> and analytical concentration of the acid are known, the extent of dissociation and pH of a solution of a monoprotic acid can be easily calculated using an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ICE_table" title="ICE table">ICE table</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Strong_acids_and_bases">Strong acids and bases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Acid_dissociation_constant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Strong acids and bases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A strong acid is effectively completely dissociated in aqueous solution below certain concentration threshold values in concentrated solutions of acids like <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a> above 2-3 M. A <a href="/wiki/Tacit_assumption" title="Tacit assumption">tacit assumption</a> occurs here about hydrogen ion concentration in dilute solutions being equal to analytic concentration of the monobasic acid as a result of total dissociation. All acids with a p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> value of less than −2 are more than 99% dissociated at pH 0 (1 M acid). This is known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leveling_effect_(chemistry)" title="Leveling effect (chemistry)">solvent leveling</a> since such acids are brought to the same level of being <i>strong acids</i>, regardless of their p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values. Likewise, all bases with a p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> value larger than the upper limit are more than 99%  dissociated at all attainable pH values and are classified as <i>strong bases</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SA_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-SA-9">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>An example of a strong acid is <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>, HCl, which has a p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> value, estimated from thermodynamic quantities, of −9.3 in water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup> The concentration of undissociated HCl molecules in aqueous solution will be less than 0.01% of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analytical_concentration" title="Analytical concentration">analytical concentration</a> of the acid. Hydrochloric acid is said to be <i>fully dissociated</i> in aqueous solution because the amount of undissociated acid, in equilibrium with the dissociation products, is below the <a href="/wiki/Detection_limit" title="Detection limit">detection limit</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polyprotic_acids">Polyprotic acids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Acid_dissociation_constant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Polyprotic acids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:H3PO4_speciation.png"><img alt="Acids with more than one ionizable hydrogen atoms are called polyprotic acids, and have multiple deprotonation states, also called species. This image plots the relative percentages of the different protonation species of phosphoric acid H 3 P O 4 as a function of solution p H. Phosphoric acid has three ionizable hydrogen atoms whose p K A's are roughly 2, 7 and 12. Below p H 2, the triply protonated species H 3 P O 4 predominates; the double protonated species H 2 P O 4 minus predominates near p H 5; the singly protonated species H P O 4 2 minus predominates near p H 9 and the unprotonated species P O 4 3 minus predominates above p H 12" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="349" data-file-width="350" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/H3PO4_speciation.png/200px-H3PO4_speciation.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/H3PO4_speciation.png/300px-H3PO4_speciation.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/16/H3PO4_speciation.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:H3PO4_speciation.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Phosphoric acid speciation</div></div></div>
<p>Polyprotic acids are acids that can lose more than one proton. The constant for dissociation of the first proton may be denoted as <i>K</i><sub>a1</sub> and the constants for dissociation of successive protons as <i>K</i><sub>a2</sub>, etc. <a href="/wiki/Phosphoric_acid" title="Phosphoric acid">Phosphoric acid</a>, H<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>, is an example of a polyprotic acid as it can lose three protons.
</p>
<dl><dd><table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Equilibrium
</th>
<th>p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> value<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[20]</a></sup>
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>H<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> ⇌ <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>PO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + H<sup>+</sup>
</td>
<td>p<i>K</i><sub>a1</sub> = 2.14
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>PO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> ⇌ <span class="chemf nowrap">HPO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + H<sup>+</sup>
</td>
<td>p<i>K</i><sub>a2</sub> = 7.20
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><span class="chemf nowrap">HPO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> ⇌ <span class="chemf nowrap">PO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + H<sup>+</sup>
</td>
<td>p<i>K</i><sub>a3</sub> = 12.37
</td></tr></tbody></table></dd></dl>
<p>When the difference between successive p<i>K</i> values is about four or more, as in this example, each species may be considered as an acid in its own right;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup> In fact salts of <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>PO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> may be crystallised from solution by adjustment of pH to about 5.5 and salts of <span class="chemf nowrap">HPO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> may be crystallised from solution by adjustment of pH to about 10. The species distribution diagram shows that the concentrations of the two ions are maximum at pH 5.5 and 10.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Citric_acid_speciation.png"><img alt="This image plots the relative percentages of the protonation species of citric acid as a function of p H. Citric acid has three ionizable hydrogen atoms and thus three p K A values. Below the lowest p K A, the triply protonated species prevails; between the lowest and middle p K A, the doubly protonated form prevails; between the middle and highest p K A, the singly protonated form prevails; and above the highest p K A, the unprotonated form of citric acid is predominant." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="248" data-file-width="242" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Citric_acid_speciation.png/200px-Citric_acid_speciation.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/39/Citric_acid_speciation.png 1.5x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Citric_acid_speciation.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>% species formation calculated with the program HySS for a 10 millimolar solution of citric acid. p<i>K</i><sub>a1</sub> = 3.13, p<i>K</i><sub>a2</sub> = 4.76, p<i>K</i><sub>a3</sub> = 6.40.</div></div></div>
<p>When the difference between successive p<i>K</i> values is less than about four there is overlap between the pH range of existence of the species in equilibrium. The smaller the difference, the more the overlap. The case of citric acid is shown at the right; solutions of citric acid are buffered over the whole range of pH 2.5 to 7.5.
</p><p>According to Pauling's first rule, successive p<i>K</i> values of a given acid increase (p<i>K</i><sub>a2</sub> &gt; p<i>K</i><sub>a1</sub>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pauling_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-pauling-24">[22]</a></sup> For oxyacids with more than one ionizable hydrogen on the same atom, the p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values often increase by about 5 units for each proton removed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miessler2ed_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miessler2ed-25">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huheey_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Huheey-26">[24]</a></sup> as in the example of phosphoric acid above.
</p><p>In the case of a diprotic acid, H<sub>2</sub>A, the two equilibria are
</p>
<dl><dd>H<sub>2</sub>A ⇌ HA<sup>−</sup> + H<sup>+</sup></dd>
<dd>HA<sup>−</sup> ⇌ A<sup>2−</sup> + H<sup>+</sup></dd></dl>
<p>it can be seen that the second proton is removed from a negatively charged species. Since the proton carries a positive charge extra work is needed to remove it; that is the cause of the trend noted above. Phosphoric acid values (above) illustrate this rule, as do the values for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vanadic_acid" title="Vanadic acid">vanadic acid</a>, H<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>. When an exception to the rule is found it indicates that a major change in structure is occurring. In the case of VO<sub>2</sub><sup>+</sup> (aq), the vanadium is <a href="/wiki/Octahedral_molecular_geometry" title="Octahedral molecular geometry">octahedral</a>, 6-coordinate, whereas vanadic acid is <a href="/wiki/Tetrahedral_molecular_geometry" title="Tetrahedral molecular geometry">tetrahedral</a>, 4-coordinate. This is the basis for an explanation of why p<i>K</i><sub>a1</sub> &gt; p<i>K</i><sub>a2</sub> for vanadium(V) oxoacids.
</p>

</td>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Isoelectric_point">Isoelectric point</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Acid_dissociation_constant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Isoelectric point">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isoelectric_point" title="Isoelectric point">isoelectric point</a></div>
<p>For substances in solution the isoelectric point (p<i>I</i>) is defined as the pH at which the sum, weighted by charge value, of concentrations of positively charged species is equal to the weighted sum of concentrations of negatively charged species. In the case that there is one species of each type, the isoelectric point can be obtained directly from the p<i>K</i> values. Take the example of <a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">glycine</a>, defined as AH. There are two dissociation equilibria to consider.
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">AH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> ⇌ AH + H<sup>+</sup>;<span class="nowrap">     </span> [AH][H<sup>+</sup>] = <i>K</i><sub>1</sub>[<span class="chemf nowrap">AH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>]</dd>
<dd>AH ⇌ A<sup>−</sup> + H<sup>+</sup>;<span class="nowrap">     </span> [A<sup>−</sup>][H<sup>+</sup>] = <i>K</i><sub>2</sub>[AH]</dd></dl>
<p>Substitute the expression for [AH] into the first equation
</p>
<dl><dd>[A<sup>−</sup>][H<sup>+</sup>]<sup>2</sup> = <i>K</i><sub>1</sub><i>K</i><sub>2</sub>[<span class="chemf nowrap">AH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>]</dd></dl>
<p>At the isoelectric point the concentration of the positively charged species, AH<sub>2</sub><sup>+</sup>, is equal to the concentration of the negatively charged species, A<sup>−</sup>, so
</p>
<dl><dd>[H<sup>+</sup>]<sup>2</sup> = <i>K</i><sub>1</sub><i>K</i><sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>Therefore, taking <a href="/wiki/Cologarithm" title="Cologarithm">cologarithms</a>, the pH is given by
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathrm {p} I={\frac {\mathrm {p} K_{1}+\mathrm {p} K_{2}}{2}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">p</mi>
</mrow>
<mi>I</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">p</mi>
</mrow>
<msub>
<mi>K</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>+</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">p</mi>
</mrow>
<msub>
<mi>K</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathrm {p} I={\frac {\mathrm {p} K_{1}+\mathrm {p} K_{2}}{2}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \mathrm {p} I={\frac {\mathrm {p} K_{1}+\mathrm {p} K_{2}}{2}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cc00e052fe0c877eb4bc071b443d0d26cb6e23fa" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:17.879ex; height:5.343ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>p<i>I</i> values for amino acids are listed at <a href="/wiki/Proteinogenic_amino_acid#Chemical_properties" title="Proteinogenic amino acid">Proteinogenic amino acid#Chemical properties</a>. When more than two charged species are in equilibrium with each other a full speciation calculation may be needed.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_self-ionization">Water self-ionization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Acid_dissociation_constant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Water self-ionization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Self-ionization_of_water" title="Self-ionization of water">Self-ionization of water</a></div>
<p>Water possesses both acidic and basic properties and is said to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphiprotic" title="Amphiprotic">amphiprotic</a>. The ionization equilibrium can be written
</p>
<dl><dd>H<sub>2</sub>O ⇌ OH<sup>−</sup> + H<sup>+</sup></dd></dl>
<p>where in aqueous solution H<sup>+</sup> or H<sup>+</sup>(aq) denotes a <a href="/wiki/Solvation" title="Solvation">solvated</a> proton. Often this is written as the <a href="/wiki/Hydronium" title="Hydronium">hydronium</a> ion H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup>, but this formula is not exact because in fact there is solvation by more than one water molecule and species such as H<sub>5</sub>O<sub>2</sub><sup>+</sup>, H<sub>7</sub>O<sub>3</sub><sup>+</sup> and H<sub>9</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup> are also present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>The equilibrium constant is given by
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {a} }=\mathrm {\frac {[H^{+}][OH^{-}]}{[H_{2}O]}} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>K</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>

</mrow>
</msup>
<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
<mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
<mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi>
<msup>
<mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
</mrow>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>

<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
</mrow>
</mfrac>

</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {a} }=\mathrm {\frac {[H^{+}][OH^{-}]}{[H_{2}O]}} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {a} }=\mathrm {\frac {[H^{+}][OH^{-}]}{[H_{2}O]}} }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5f1c4928429f6b59315a3a5aab2274f92cf6d5bb" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:17.865ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>When, as is usually the case, the concentration of water can be assumed to be constant, this expression may be replaced by
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {w} }=[\mathrm {H} ^{+}][\mathrm {OH} ^{-}]\,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>+</mo>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
<mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
<msup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>

<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {w} }=[\mathrm {H} ^{+}][\mathrm {OH} ^{-}]\,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {w} }=[\mathrm {H} ^{+}][\mathrm {OH} ^{-}]\,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/237c26311611aaaafb80c4fdba015d381730e13d" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:17.781ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Self-ionization_of_water" title="Self-ionization of water">self-ionization</a> constant of water, <i>K</i><sub>w</sub>, is thus just a special case of an acid dissociation constant. A logarithmic form analogous to p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> may also be defined

<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathrm {p} K_{\mathrm {w} }=-\log _{10}\left(K_{\mathrm {w} }\right)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</msub>

<mo>(</mo>

<p>These data can be fitted to a parabola with
</p>
<dl><dd>p<i>K</i><sub>w</sub> = 14.94 − 0.04209<i>T</i> + 0.0001718<i>T</i><sup>2</sup></dd></dl>
<p>From this equation, p<i>K</i><sub>w</sub> = 14 at 24.87 °C. At that temperature both hydrogen and hydroxide ions have a concentration of 10<sup>−7</sup> M.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protonation_constants">Protonation constants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Acid_dissociation_constant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Protonation constants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The dissociation of a monoprotic acid can also be described as the protonation of the conjugate base of the acid
</p>
<dl><dd>A<sup>−</sup> + H<sup>+</sup> ⇌ AH</dd></dl>
<p>This leads to the definition of an <i>association</i> (protonation) constant, denoted here as K<sub>association</sub>, as
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {association} }=\mathrm {\frac {[HA]}{[A^{-}][H^{+}]}} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>K</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">o</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">c</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">t</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">o</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">n</mi>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
<mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
</mrow>
<mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
<msup>
<mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
<mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
<msup>
<mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>+</mo>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {association} }=\mathrm {\frac {[HA]}{[A^{-}][H^{+}]}} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {association} }=\mathrm {\frac {[HA]}{[A^{-}][H^{+}]}} }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5186b62deadd7bdb67ba155879c54c7aa6a54d7f" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:23.017ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>The <i>dissociation</i> (deprotonation) constant definition can be written as
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {dissociation} }=\mathrm {\frac {[A^{-}][H^{+}]}{[HA]}} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>K</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">o</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">c</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">t</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">o</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">n</mi>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
<msup>
<mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
<mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
<msup>
<mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>+</mo>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
</mrow>
<mrow>
<mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
<mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {dissociation} }=\mathrm {\frac {[A^{-}][H^{+}]}{[HA]}} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {dissociation} }=\mathrm {\frac {[A^{-}][H^{+}]}{[HA]}} }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b9416eae98b0914a5701dad3345e2325482a28f3" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:23.567ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>The definitions show that the values of the two constants are reciprocals of each other and
</p>
<dl><dd>pK<sub>dissociation</sub> = log(K<sub>association</sub>)</dd></dl>
<p>The situation is a little more complicated with polybasic acids. For example, with phosphoric acid
</p>
<dl><dd>pK<sub>a1</sub> = log(K<sub>association,3</sub>)</dd>
<dd>pK<sub>a2</sub> = log(K<sub>association,2</sub>)</dd>
<dd>pK<sub>a3</sub> = log(K<sub>association,1</sub>)</dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Amphoteric_substances">Amphoteric substances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Acid_dissociation_constant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Amphoteric substances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphoteric" title="Amphoteric">amphoteric</a> substance is one that can act as an acid or as a base, depending on pH. Water (above) is amphoteric. Another example of an amphoteric molecule is the <a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">bicarbonate</a> ion <span class="chemf nowrap">HCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> that is the conjugate base of the <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">carbonic acid</a> molecule H<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> in the equilibrium
</p>
<dl><dd>H<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>O ⇌ <span class="chemf nowrap">HCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> + H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup></dd></dl>
<p>but also the conjugate acid of the <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a> ion <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> in (the reverse of) the equilibrium
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">HCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> + OH<sup>−</sup> ⇌ <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> + H<sub>2</sub>O.</dd></dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">Carbonic acid</a> equilibria are important for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acid-base_homeostasis" title="Acid-base homeostasis">acid–base homeostasis</a> in the human body.
</p><p>An <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acid</a> is also amphoteric with the added complication that the neutral molecule is subject to an internal acid–base equilibrium in which the basic amino group attracts and binds the proton from the acidic carboxyl group, forming a <a href="/wiki/Zwitterion" title="Zwitterion">zwitterion</a>.
</p>
<dl><dd>NH<sub>2</sub>CHRCO<sub>2</sub>H ⇌ <span class="chemf nowrap">NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>CHRCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>At pH less than about 5 both the carboxylate group and the amino group are protonated. As pH increases the acid dissociates according to
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>CHRCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>H</span> ⇌ <span class="chemf nowrap">NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>CHRCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + H<sup>+</sup></dd></dl>
<p>At high pH a second dissociation may take place.
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>CHRCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> ⇌ <span class="chemf nowrap">NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>CHRCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + H<sup>+</sup></dd></dl>
<p>Thus the zwitterion, <span class="chemf nowrap">NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>CHRCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, is amphoteric because it may either be protonated or deprotonated.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bases_and_basicity">Bases and basicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Acid_dissociation_constant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Bases and basicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The equilibrium constant <i>K</i><sub>b</sub> for a base is usually defined as the <i>association</i> constant for protonation of the base, B, to form the conjugate acid, HB<sup>+</sup>.
</p>
<dl><dd>B + H<sub>2</sub>O ⇌ HB<sup>+</sup> + OH<sup>−</sup></dd></dl>
<p>Using similar reasoning to that used before

<p><i>K</i><sub>b</sub> is related to <i>K</i><sub>a</sub> for the conjugate acid. </p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathrm {[OH^{-}]} ={\frac {K_{\mathrm {w} }}{\mathrm {[H^{+}]} }}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {b} }={\frac {[\mathrm {HB^{+}} ]K_{\mathrm {w} }}{\mathrm {[B][H^{+}]} }}={\frac {K_{\mathrm {w} }}{K_{\mathrm {a} }}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {b} }={\frac {[\mathrm {HB^{+}} ]K_{\mathrm {w} }}{\mathrm {[B][H^{+}]} }}={\frac {K_{\mathrm {w} }}{K_{\mathrm {a} }}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle K_{\mathrm {b} }={\frac {[\mathrm {HB^{+}} ]K_{\mathrm {w} }}{\mathrm {[B][H^{+}]} }}={\frac {K_{\mathrm {w} }}{K_{\mathrm {a} }}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/55e99b9289faff1c69f7cc5b289e461e363515a5" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:23.965ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>When <i>K</i><sub>a</sub>, <i>K</i><sub>b</sub> and <i>K</i><sub>w</sub> are determined under the same conditions of temperature and ionic strength, it follows, taking <a href="/wiki/Cologarithm" title="Cologarithm">cologarithms</a>, that p<i>K</i><sub>b</sub> = p<i>K</i><sub>w</sub> − p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub>. In aqueous solutions at 25 °C, p<i>K</i><sub>w</sub> is 13.9965,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crc_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-crc-29">[27]</a></sup> so
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle pK_{\mathrm {b} }\approx 14-pK_{\mathrm {a} }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>p</mi>
<msub>
<mi>K</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">b</mi>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>≈<!-- ≈ --></mo>
<mn>14</mn>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>p</mi>
<msub>
<mi>K</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle pK_{\mathrm {b} }\approx 14-pK_{\mathrm {a} }}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle pK_{\mathrm {b} }\approx 14-pK_{\mathrm {a} }}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8a9fba4dc241ede801bd3a96e179429cb3961bfb" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; margin-left: -0.089ex; width:16.839ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>with sufficient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accuracy" title="Accuracy">accuracy</a> for most practical purposes. In effect there is no need to define p<i>K</i><sub>b</sub> separately from p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub>, but it is done here as often only p<i>K</i><sub>b</sub> values can be found in the older literature.
</p><p>For <a href="/wiki/Metal_hydroxide" title="Metal hydroxide">metal hydroxides</a> <i>K</i><sub>b</sub> can also be defined as the <i>dissociation</i> constant for loss of a hydroxide ion: <span class="nowrap">B(OH) ⇌ B<sup>+</sup> + OH<sup>−</sup></span> or <span class="nowrap">B(OH)<sub>2</sub> ⇌ B(OH)<sup>+</sup> + OH<sup>−</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup> This is the reciprocal of a <a href="/wiki/Stability_constants_of_complexes" title="Stability constants of complexes">stability constant</a> for formation of the complex.
</p><p>Because the relationship p<i>K</i><sub>b</sub> = p<i>K</i><sub>w</sub> − p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> holds only in aqueous solutions (though analogous relationships apply for other solvents), subdisciplines of chemistry like <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a> that usually deal with nonaqueous solutions generally do not use p<i>K</i><sub>b</sub> as a measure of basicity.  Instead, the p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> of the conjugate acid, denoted by p<i>K</i><sub>aH</sub>, is quoted when basicity needs to be quantified.  A higher value for p<i>K</i><sub>aH</sub> corresponds to a stronger base.  For example, the values p<i>K</i><sub>aH</sub>(C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub>N) = 5.25 and p<i>K</i><sub>aH</sub>((CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>N) = 10.75 indicate that triethylamine is a stronger base than pyridine.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temperature_dependence">Temperature dependence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Acid_dissociation_constant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Temperature dependence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>All equilibrium constants vary with <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a> according to the <a href="/wiki/Van_%27t_Hoff_equation" title="Van 't Hoff equation">van 't Hoff equation</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\frac {\operatorname {d} \ln \left(K\right)}{\operatorname {d} T}}={\frac {\Delta H^{\ominus }}{RT^{2}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\frac {\operatorname {d} \ln \left(K\right)}{\operatorname {d} T}}={\frac {\Delta H^{\ominus }}{RT^{2}}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a95e7299c21f228d1740503caf5018b41bf79097" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:17.815ex; height:6.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p><i>R</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Gas_constant" title="Gas constant">gas constant</a> and <i>T</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">absolute temperature</a> . Thus, for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exothermic" title="Exothermic">exothermic</a> reactions, (the standard <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enthalpy_change" title="Enthalpy change">enthalpy change</a>, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \Delta H^{\ominus }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<msup>

<mo>⊖<!-- ⊖ --></mo>
</mrow>

</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \Delta H^{\ominus }}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \Delta H^{\ominus }}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e7ca2ac7d655e35bf2076604abc115a3fe696542" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:5.55ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>, is negative) <i>K</i> decreases with temperature, but for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endothermic" title="Endothermic">endothermic</a> reactions (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \Delta H^{\ominus }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p><p>The standard enthalpy change for a reaction is itself a function of temperature, according to <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Kirchhoff#Kirchhoff's_law_of_thermochemistry" title="Gustav Kirchhoff">Kirchhoff's law of thermochemistry</a>:

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \left({\frac {\partial \Delta H}{\partial T}}\right)_{p}=\Delta C_{p}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \left({\frac {\partial \Delta H}{\partial T}}\right)_{p}=\Delta C_{p}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d06ed13b448cfda5772760db7d3868f4678ab082" style="vertical-align: -2.838ex; width:18.389ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>where <i>ΔC</i><sub><i>p</i></sub> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_heat_capacity" title="Specific heat capacity">heat capacity</a> change at constant pressure. In practice <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \Delta H^{\ominus }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi mathvariant="normal">Δ<!-- Δ --></mi>
<msup>
<mi>H</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>⊖<!-- ⊖ --></mo>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \Delta H^{\ominus }}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \Delta H^{\ominus }}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e7ca2ac7d655e35bf2076604abc115a3fe696542" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:5.55ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> may be taken to be constant over a small temperature range.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Acidity_in_nonaqueous_solutions">Acidity in nonaqueous solutions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Acid_dissociation_constant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Acidity in nonaqueous solutions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A solvent will be more likely to promote ionization of a dissolved acidic molecule in the following circumstances:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loudon_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-loudon-32">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>It is a <a href="/wiki/Protic_solvent" title="Protic solvent">protic solvent</a>, capable of forming hydrogen bonds.</li>
<li>It has a high <a href="/wiki/Donor_number" title="Donor number">donor number</a>, making it a strong <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_base" title="Lewis base">Lewis base</a>.</li>
<li>It has a high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dielectric_constant" title="Dielectric constant">dielectric constant</a> (relative permittivity), making it a good solvent for ionic species.</li></ol>
<p>p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values of organic compounds are often obtained using the aprotic solvents <a href="/wiki/Dimethyl_sulfoxide" title="Dimethyl sulfoxide">dimethyl sulfoxide</a> (DMSO)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loudon_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-loudon-32">[30]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Acetonitrile" title="Acetonitrile">acetonitrile</a> (ACN).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Solvent properties at 25 °C
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Solvent</th>

<th>Dielectric constant<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loudon_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-loudon-32">[30]</a></sup>
</th></tr>

<td>Water</td>
<td>18</td>
<td>78
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>DMSO is widely used as an alternative to water because it has a lower dielectric constant than water, and is less polar and so dissolves non-polar, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrophobic" title="Hydrophobic">hydrophobic</a> substances more easily. It has a measurable p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> range of about 1 to 30. Acetonitrile is less basic than DMSO, and, so, in general, acids are weaker and bases are stronger in this solvent. Some p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values at 25 °C for acetonitrile (ACN)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ivo_AN_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ivo_AN-35">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup> and dimethyl sulfoxide (DMSO)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup> are shown in the following tables. Values for water are included for comparison.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
