<p>Ionization of acids is less in an acidic solvent than in water. For example, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chloride" title="Hydrogen chloride">hydrogen chloride</a> is a weak acid when dissolved in <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a>. This is because acetic acid is a much weaker base than water.
</p>
<dl><dd>HCl + CH<sub>3</sub>CO<sub>2</sub>H ⇌ Cl<sup>−</sup> + <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>C(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd>
<dd>acid + base ⇌ conjugate base + conjugate acid</dd></dl>
<p>Compare this reaction with what happens when acetic acid is dissolved in the more acidic solvent pure sulfuric acid<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> + CH<sub>3</sub>CO<sub>2</sub>H ⇌ <span class="chemf nowrap">HSO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>C(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>The unlikely <a href="/wiki/Diol" title="Diol">geminal diol</a> species <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>C(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is stable in these environments. For aqueous solutions the <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> scale is the most convenient <a href="/wiki/Acidity_function" title="Acidity function">acidity function</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup> Other acidity functions have been proposed for non-aqueous media, the most notable being the <a href="/wiki/Hammett_acidity_function" title="Hammett acidity function">Hammett acidity function</a>, <i>H</i><sub>0</sub>, for <a href="/wiki/Superacid" title="Superacid">superacid</a> media and its modified version <i>H</i><sub>−</sub> for <a href="/wiki/Superbase" title="Superbase">superbasic</a> media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carboxylic_acid_dimers.png"><img alt="This image illustrates how two carboxylic acids, C O O H, can associate through mutual hydrogen bonds. The hydroxyl portion O H of each molecule forms a hydrogen bond to the carbonyl portion C O of the other." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="827" data-file-width="1803" height="101" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Carboxylic_acid_dimers.png/220px-Carboxylic_acid_dimers.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Carboxylic_acid_dimers.png/330px-Carboxylic_acid_dimers.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Carboxylic_acid_dimers.png/440px-Carboxylic_acid_dimers.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carboxylic_acid_dimers.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dimerization of a carboxylic acid.</div></div></div>
<p>In aprotic solvents, <a href="/wiki/Oligomer" title="Oligomer">oligomers</a>, such as the well-known acetic acid <a href="/wiki/Dimer_(chemistry)" title="Dimer (chemistry)">dimer</a>, may be formed by hydrogen bonding. An acid may also form hydrogen bonds to its conjugate base. This process, known as <a href="/wiki/Homoassociation" title="Homoassociation">homoconjugation</a>, has the effect of enhancing the acidity of acids, lowering their effective p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values, by stabilizing the conjugate base. Homoconjugation enhances the proton-donating power of toluenesulfonic acid in acetonitrile solution by a factor of nearly 800.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup> In aqueous solutions, homoconjugation does not occur, because water forms stronger hydrogen bonds to the conjugate base than does the acid.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mixed_solvents">Mixed solvents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Acid_dissociation_constant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Mixed solvents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Acetic_acid_pK_dioxane_water.png"><img alt="The p K A of acetic acid in the mixed solvent dioxane/water. p K A increases as the proportion of dioxane increases, primarily because the dielectric constant of the mixture decreases with increasing doxane content. A lower dielectric constant disfavors the dissociation of the uncharged acid into the charged ions, H + and C H 3 C O O minus, shifting the equilibrium to favor the uncharged protonated form C H 3 C O O H. Since the protonated form is the reactant not the product of the dissociation, this shift decreases the equilibrium constant K A, and increases P K A, its negative logarithm." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="269" data-file-width="336" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Acetic_acid_pK_dioxane_water.png/220px-Acetic_acid_pK_dioxane_water.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Acetic_acid_pK_dioxane_water.png/330px-Acetic_acid_pK_dioxane_water.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a9/Acetic_acid_pK_dioxane_water.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Acetic_acid_pK_dioxane_water.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> of acetic acid in dioxane/water mixtures. Data at 25 °C from Pine <i>et al.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>When a compound has limited solubility in water it is common practice (in the pharmaceutical industry, for example) to determine p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values in a solvent mixture such as water/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dioxane" title="Dioxane">dioxane</a> or water/<a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>, in which the compound is more soluble.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup> In the example shown at the right, the p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> value rises steeply with increasing percentage of dioxane as the dielectric constant of the mixture is decreasing.
</p><p>A p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> value obtained in a mixed solvent cannot be used directly for aqueous solutions. The reason for this is that when the solvent is in its standard state its activity is <i>defined</i> as one. For example, the standard state of water:dioxane mixture with 9:1 <a href="/wiki/Mixing_ratio" title="Mixing ratio">mixing ratio</a> is precisely that solvent mixture, with no added solutes. To obtain the p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> value for use with aqueous solutions it has to be extrapolated to zero co-solvent concentration from values obtained from various co-solvent mixtures.
</p><p>These facts are obscured by the omission of the solvent from the expression that is normally used to define p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub>, but p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values obtained in a <i>given</i> mixed solvent can be compared to each other, giving relative acid strengths. The same is true of p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values obtained in a particular non-aqueous solvent such a DMSO.
</p><p>As of 2008, a universal, solvent-independent, scale for acid dissociation constants has not been developed, since there is no known way to compare the standard states of two different solvents.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Factors_that_affect_pKa_values">Factors that affect p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Acid_dissociation_constant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Factors that affect pKa values">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Pauling's second rule is that the value of the first p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> for acids of the formula XO<sub><i>m</i></sub>(OH)<sub><i>n</i></sub> depends primarily on the number of oxo groups <i>m</i>, and is approximately independent of the number of hydroxy groups <i>n</i>, and also of the central atom X. Approximate values of p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> are 8 for <i>m</i> = 0, 2 for <i>m</i> = 1, −3 for <i>m</i> = 2 and &lt; −10 for <i>m</i> = 3.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pauling_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-pauling-24">[22]</a></sup> Alternatively, various numerical formulas have been proposed including p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> = 8 − 5<i>m</i> (known as <a href="/wiki/Ronnie_Bell" title="Ronnie Bell">Bell's</a> rule),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miessler2ed_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Miessler2ed-25">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-House2_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-House2-44">[42]</a></sup> p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> = 7 − 5<i>m</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huheey_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Huheey-26">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Douglas_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Douglas-45">[43]</a></sup> or p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> = 9 − 7<i>m</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miessler2ed_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-Miessler2ed-25">[23]</a></sup> The dependence on <i>m</i> correlates with the oxidation state of the central atom, X: the higher the oxidation state the stronger the oxyacid. For example, p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> for HClO is 7.2, for HClO<sub>2</sub> is 2.0, for HClO<sub>3</sub> is −1 and HClO<sub>4</sub> is a strong acid (<span class="nowrap">p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> ≪ 0</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SA_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-SA-9">[7]</a></sup> The increased acidity on adding an oxo group is due to stabilization of the conjugate base by delocalization of its negative charge over an additional oxygen atom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-House2_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-House2-44">[42]</a></sup> This rule can help assign molecular structure: for example <a href="/wiki/Phosphorous_acid" title="Phosphorous acid">phosphorous acid</a> (H<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>3</sub>) has a p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> near 2 suggested that the structure is HPO(OH)<sub>2</sub>, as later confirmed by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_resonance_spectroscopy" title="Nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy">NMR spectroscopy</a>, and not P(OH)<sub>3</sub> which would be expected to have a p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> near 8.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Douglas_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Douglas-45">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fumaric-acid-2D-skeletal.png"><img alt="Fumaric acid consists of two double-bonded carbon atoms capped on both sides by carboxylic acid groups C O O H; thus, its chemical formula is C O O H C H C H C O O H. The molecule has two ionizable hydrogen atoms and thus two p K As. The central double bond is in the trans configuration, which holds the two carboxylate groups apart. This contrasts with the cis isomer, maleic acid." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="627" data-file-width="1100" height="57" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Fumaric-acid-2D-skeletal.png/100px-Fumaric-acid-2D-skeletal.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Fumaric-acid-2D-skeletal.png/150px-Fumaric-acid-2D-skeletal.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Fumaric-acid-2D-skeletal.png/200px-Fumaric-acid-2D-skeletal.png 2x" width="100"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fumaric-acid-2D-skeletal.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fumaric acid</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maleic-acid-2D-skeletal-A.svg"><img alt="Maleic acid consists of two double-bonded carbon atoms capped on both sides by carboxylic acid groups C O O H; thus, its chemical formula is C O O H C H C H C O O H. It has two ionizable hydrogen atoms and thus two p K As. The central double bond is in the cis configuration. This holds the two carboxylate groups close enough so that when one group is protonated and the other deprotonated, a strong hydrogen bond can be formed between the two groups. This makes the mono-protonated species much more stable than the corresponding species of the trans isomer, fumaric acid." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="58" data-file-width="141" height="41" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Maleic-acid-2D-skeletal-A.svg/100px-Maleic-acid-2D-skeletal-A.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Maleic-acid-2D-skeletal-A.svg/150px-Maleic-acid-2D-skeletal-A.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Maleic-acid-2D-skeletal-A.svg/200px-Maleic-acid-2D-skeletal-A.svg.png 2x" width="100"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maleic-acid-2D-skeletal-A.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Maleic acid</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Proton_sponge.svg"><img alt="Proton sponge is a derivative of naphthalene with dimethylamino groups in the one and ten positions. This brings the two dimethyl amino groups into close proximity to each other." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="110" data-file-width="109" height="101" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Proton_sponge.svg/100px-Proton_sponge.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Proton_sponge.svg/150px-Proton_sponge.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Proton_sponge.svg/200px-Proton_sponge.svg.png 2x" width="100"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Proton_sponge.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Proton sponge</div></div></div>
<p>With organic acids <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inductive_effects" title="Inductive effects">inductive effects</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mesomeric_effect" title="Mesomeric effect">mesomeric effects</a> affect the p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values. A simple example is provided by the effect of replacing the hydrogen atoms in acetic acid by the more electronegative chlorine atom. The electron-withdrawing effect of the substituent makes ionisation easier, so successive p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values decrease in the series 4.7, 2.8, 1.4, and 0.7 when 0, 1, 2, or 3 chlorine atoms are present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hammett_equation" title="Hammett equation">Hammett equation</a>, provides a general expression for the effect of substituents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>log(<i>K</i><sub>a</sub>) = log(<i>K</i><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">0</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">a</sub></span>) + <i>ρσ</i>.</dd></dl>
<p><i>K</i><sub>a</sub> is the dissociation constant of a substituted compound, <i>K</i><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">0</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">a</sub></span> is the dissociation constant when the substituent is hydrogen, <i>ρ</i> is a property of the unsubstituted compound and <i>σ</i> has a particular value for each substituent. A plot of log(<i>K</i><sub>a</sub>) against <i>σ</i> is a straight line with <a href="/wiki/Y-intercept" title="Y-intercept">intercept</a> log(<i>K</i><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">0</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">a</sub></span>) and <a href="/wiki/Slope" title="Slope">slope</a> <i>ρ</i>. This is an example of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linear_free_energy_relationship" title="Linear free energy relationship">linear free energy relationship</a> as log(<i>K</i><sub>a</sub>) is proportional to the standard free energy change. Hammett originally<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup> formulated the relationship with data from <a href="/wiki/Benzoic_acid" title="Benzoic acid">benzoic acid</a> with different substiuents in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arene_substitution_patterns" title="Arene substitution patterns">ortho</a>-</i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arene_substitution_patterns" title="Arene substitution patterns">para</a>-</i> positions: some numerical values are in <a href="/wiki/Hammett_equation" title="Hammett equation">Hammett equation</a>. This and other studies allowed substituents to be ordered according to their <a href="/wiki/Inductive_effect" title="Inductive effect">electron-withdrawing</a> or <a href="/wiki/Inductive_effect" title="Inductive effect">electron-releasing</a> power, and to distinguish between inductive and mesomeric effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">Alcohols</a> do not normally behave as acids in water, but the presence of a double bond adjacent to the OH group can substantially decrease the p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> by the mechanism of <a href="/wiki/Keto%E2%80%93enol_tautomerism" title="Keto–enol tautomerism">keto–enol tautomerism</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascorbic_acid" title="Ascorbic acid">Ascorbic acid</a> is an example of this effect. The diketone 2,4-pentanedione (<a href="/wiki/Acetylacetone" title="Acetylacetone">acetylacetone</a>) is also a weak acid because of the keto–enol equilibrium. In aromatic compounds, such as <a href="/wiki/Phenol" title="Phenol">phenol</a>, which have an OH substituent, <a href="/wiki/Conjugated_system" title="Conjugated system">conjugation</a> with the aromatic ring as a whole greatly increases the stability of the deprotonated form.
</p><p>Structural effects can also be important. The difference between <a href="/wiki/Fumaric_acid" title="Fumaric acid">fumaric acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maleic_acid" title="Maleic acid">maleic acid</a> is a classic example. Fumaric acid is (E)-1,4-but-2-enedioic acid, a <i>trans</i> <a href="/wiki/Isomer" title="Isomer">isomer</a>, whereas maleic acid is the corresponding <i>cis</i> isomer, i.e. (Z)-1,4-but-2-enedioic acid (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cis-trans_isomerism" title="Cis-trans isomerism">cis-trans isomerism</a>). Fumaric acid has p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values of approximately 3.0 and 4.5. By contrast, maleic acid has p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values of approximately 1.5 and 6.5. The reason for this large difference is that when one proton is removed from the <i>cis</i> isomer (maleic acid) a strong <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intramolecular" title="Intramolecular">intramolecular</a> <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bond</a> is formed with the nearby remaining carboxyl group. This favors the formation of the maleate H<sup>+</sup>, and it opposes the removal of the second proton from that species. In the <i>trans</i> isomer, the two carboxyl groups are always far apart, so hydrogen bonding is not observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton_sponge" title="Proton sponge">Proton sponge</a>, 1,8-bis(dimethylamino)naphthalene, has a p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> value of 12.1. It is one of the strongest amine bases known. The high basicity is attributed to the relief of strain upon protonation and strong internal hydrogen bonding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Effects of the solvent and solvation should be mentioned also in this section. It turns out, these influences are more subtle than that of a dielectric medium mentioned above. For example, the expected (by electronic effects of methyl substituents) and observed in gas phase order of basicity of methylamines, Me<sub>3</sub>N &gt; Me<sub>2</sub>NH &gt; MeNH<sub>2</sub> &gt; NH<sub>3</sub>, is changed by water to Me<sub>2</sub>NH &gt; MeNH<sub>2</sub> &gt; Me<sub>3</sub>N &gt; NH<sub>3</sub>. Neutral methylamine molecules are hydrogen-bonded to water molecules mainly through one acceptor, N–HOH, interaction and only occasionally just one more donor bond, NH–OH<sub>2</sub>. Hence, methylamines are stabilized to about the same extent by hydration, regardless of the number of methyl groups. In stark contrast, corresponding methylammonium cations always utilize <b>all</b> the available protons for donor NH–OH<sub>2</sub> bonding. Relative stabilization of methylammonium ions thus decreases with the number of methyl groups explaining the order of water basicity of methylamines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RMC_2013_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-RMC_2013-6">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermodynamics">Thermodynamics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Acid_dissociation_constant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Thermodynamics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An equilibrium constant is related to the standard <a href="/wiki/Gibbs_free_energy" title="Gibbs free energy">Gibbs energy</a> change for the reaction, so for an acid dissociation constant
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \Delta G^{\ominus }=-RT\ln K_{\mathrm {a} }\approx 2.303RT\ \mathrm {p} K_{\mathrm {a} }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \Delta G^{\ominus }=-RT\ln K_{\mathrm {a} }\approx 2.303RT\ \mathrm {p} K_{\mathrm {a} }}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ac56372bd108a3ce988d08b7e925c933aea0f2d2" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:36.017ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>.</dd></dl>
<p><i>R</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Gas_constant" title="Gas constant">gas constant</a> and <i>T</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">absolute temperature</a>. Note that <span class="nowrap">p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> = −log(<i>K</i><sub>a</sub>)</span> and <span class="nowrap">2.303 ≈ <a href="/wiki/Natural_logarithm" title="Natural logarithm">ln</a>(10)</span>. At 25 °C, Δ<i>G</i><sup>⊖</sup> in kJ·mol<sup>−1</sup> ≈ 5.708 p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> (1 kJ·mol<sup>−1</sup> = 1000 <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">joules</a> per <a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">mole</a>). Free energy is made up of an <a href="/wiki/Enthalpy" title="Enthalpy">enthalpy</a> term and an <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a> term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldmine_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goldmine-13">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \Delta G^{\ominus }=\Delta H^{\ominus }-T\Delta S^{\ominus }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<p>The standard enthalpy change can be determined by <a href="/wiki/Calorimetry" title="Calorimetry">calorimetry</a> or by using the <a href="/wiki/Van_%27t_Hoff_equation" title="Van 't Hoff equation">van 't Hoff equation</a>, though the calorimetric method is preferable. When both the standard enthalpy change and acid dissociation constant have been determined, the standard entropy change is easily calculated from the equation above. In the following table, the entropy terms are calculated from the experimental values of p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> and Δ<i>H</i><sup>⊖</sup>. The data were critically selected and refer to 25 °C and zero ionic strength, in water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldmine_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Goldmine-13">[11]</a></sup>

<p>The first point to note is that, when p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> is positive, the standard free energy change for the dissociation reaction is also positive. Second, some reactions are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exothermic" title="Exothermic">exothermic</a> and some are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endothermic" title="Endothermic">endothermic</a>, but, when Δ<i>H</i><sup>⊖</sup> is negative <i>T</i>ΔS<sup>⊖</sup> is the dominant factor, which determines that Δ<i>G</i><sup>⊖</sup> is positive. Last, the entropy contribution is always unfavourable (<span class="nowrap">Δ<i>S</i><sup>⊖</sup> &lt; 0</span>) in these reactions. Ions in aqueous solution tend to orient the surrounding water molecules, which orders the solution and decreases the entropy. The contribution of an ion to the entropy is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Partial_molar_quantity" title="Partial molar quantity">partial molar</a> entropy which is often negative, especially for small or highly charged ions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[52]</a></sup> The ionization of a neutral acid involves formation of two ions so that the entropy decreases (<span class="nowrap">Δ<i>S</i><sup>⊖</sup> &lt; 0</span>). On the second ionization of the same acid, there are now three ions and the anion has a charge, so the entropy again decreases.
</p><p>Note that the <i>standard</i> free energy change for the reaction is for the changes <i>from</i> the reactants in their standard states <i>to</i> the products in their standard states. The free energy change <i>at</i> equilibrium is zero since the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_potential" title="Chemical potential">chemical potentials</a> of reactants and products are equal at equilibrium.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Experimental_determination">Experimental determination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Acid_dissociation_constant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Experimental determination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oxalic_acid_titration_grid.png"><img alt=" The image shows the titration curve of oxalic acid, showing the pH of the solution as a function of added base. There is a small inflection point at about pH 3 and then a large jump from pH 5 to pH 11, followed by another region of slowly increasing pH." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Oxalic_acid_titration_grid.png/220px-Oxalic_acid_titration_grid.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Oxalic_acid_titration_grid.png/330px-Oxalic_acid_titration_grid.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Oxalic_acid_titration_grid.png/440px-Oxalic_acid_titration_grid.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oxalic_acid_titration_grid.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A calculated <a href="/wiki/Titration_curve" title="Titration curve">titration curve</a> of <a href="/wiki/Oxalic_acid" title="Oxalic acid">oxalic acid</a> titrated with a solution of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">sodium hydroxide</a></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Determination_of_equilibrium_constants" title="Determination of equilibrium constants">Determination of equilibrium constants</a></div>
<p>The experimental determination of p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values is commonly performed by means of <a href="/wiki/Titration" title="Titration">titrations</a>, in a medium of high ionic strength and at constant temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[53]</a></sup> A typical procedure would be as follows. A solution of the compound in the medium is acidified with a strong acid to the point where the compound is fully protonated. The solution is then titrated with a strong base until all the protons have been removed. At each point in the titration pH is measured using a <a href="/wiki/Glass_electrode" title="Glass electrode">glass electrode</a> and a <a href="/wiki/PH_meter" title="PH meter">pH meter</a>. The equilibrium constants are found by fitting calculated pH values to the observed values, using the method of <a href="/wiki/Least_squares" title="Least squares">least squares</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>The total volume of added strong base should be small compared to the initial volume of titrand solution in order to keep the ionic strength nearly constant. This will ensure that p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> remains invariant during the titration.
</p><p>A calculated <a href="/wiki/Titration_curve" title="Titration curve">titration curve</a> for oxalic acid is shown at the right. Oxalic acid has p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values of 1.27 and 4.27. Therefore, the buffer regions will be centered at about pH 1.3 and pH 4.3. The buffer regions carry the information necessary to get the p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values as the concentrations of acid and conjugate base change along a buffer region.
</p><p>Between the two buffer regions there is an end-point, or <a href="/wiki/Equivalence_point" title="Equivalence point">equivalence point</a>, at about pH 3. This end-point is not sharp and is typical of a diprotic acid whose buffer regions overlap by a small amount: p<i>K</i><sub>a2</sub> − p<i>K</i><sub>a1</sub> is about three in this example. (If the difference in p<i>K</i> values were about two or less, the end-point would not be noticeable.) The second end-point begins at about pH  6.3 and is sharp. This indicates that all the protons have been removed. When this is so, the solution is not buffered and the pH rises steeply on addition of a small amount of strong base. However, the pH does not continue to rise indefinitely. A new buffer region begins at about pH 11 (p<i>K</i><sub>w</sub> − 3), which is where <a href="/wiki/Self-ionization_of_water" title="Self-ionization of water">self-ionization of water</a> becomes important.
</p><p>It is very difficult to measure pH values of less than two in aqueous solution with a <a href="/wiki/Glass_electrode#Range_of_a_pH_glass_electrode" title="Glass electrode">glass electrode</a>, because the <a href="/wiki/Nernst_equation" title="Nernst equation">Nernst equation</a> breaks down at such low pH values. To determine p<i>K</i> values of less than about 2 or more than about 11 <a href="/wiki/Determination_of_equilibrium_constants#Absorbance" title="Determination of equilibrium constants">spectrophotometric</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[55]</a></sup>
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[56]</a></sup>
or <a href="/wiki/Determination_of_equilibrium_constants#NMR_chemical_shift_measurements" title="Determination of equilibrium constants">NMR</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUPAC-NMR_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-IUPAC-NMR-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[57]</a></sup> measurements may be used instead of, or combined with, pH measurements.
</p><p>When the glass electrode cannot be employed, as with non-aqueous solutions, spectrophotometric methods are frequently used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ivo_AN_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ivo_AN-35">[33]</a></sup> These may involve <a href="/wiki/Absorbance" title="Absorbance">absorbance</a> or <a href="/wiki/Fluorescence" title="Fluorescence">fluorescence</a> measurements. In both cases the measured quantity is assumed to be proportional to the sum of contributions from each photo-active species; with absorbance measurements the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beer-Lambert_law" title="Beer-Lambert law">Beer-Lambert law</a> is assumed to apply.
</p><p>Aqueous solutions with normal water cannot be used for <sup>1</sup>H NMR measurements but <a href="/wiki/Heavy_water" title="Heavy water">heavy water</a>, D<sub>2</sub>O, must be used instead. <sup>13</sup>C NMR data, however, can be used with normal water and <sup>1</sup>H NMR spectra can be used with non-aqueous media. The quantities measured with NMR are time-averaged <a href="/wiki/Chemical_shift" title="Chemical shift">chemical shifts</a>, as proton exchange is fast on the NMR time-scale. Other chemical shifts, such as those of <sup>31</sup>P can be measured.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Micro-constants">Micro-constants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Acid_dissociation_constant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Micro-constants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spermine.svg"><img alt="Spermine is a long, symmetrical molecule capped at both ends with amino groups N H 2. It has two N H groups symmetrically placed within the molecule, separated from each other by four methylene groups C H 2, and from the amino ends by three methylene groups. Thus, the full molecular formula is N H 2 C H 2 C H 2 C H 2 N H C H 2 C H 2 C H 2 C H 2 N H C H 2 C H 2 C H 2 N H 2." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="85" data-file-width="420" height="45" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Spermine.svg/220px-Spermine.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Spermine.svg/330px-Spermine.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Spermine.svg/440px-Spermine.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spermine.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spermine</div></div></div>
<p>A base such as <a href="/wiki/Spermine" title="Spermine">spermine</a> has a few different sites where protonation can occur. In this example the first proton can go on the terminal –NH<sub>2</sub> group, or either of the internal –NH– groups. The p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values for dissociation of spermine protonated at one or other of the sites are examples of <a href="/wiki/Equilibrium_constant#Micro-constants" title="Equilibrium constant">micro-constants</a>. They cannot be determined directly by means of pH, absorbance, fluorescence or NMR measurements. Nevertheless, the site of protonation is very important for biological function, so mathematical methods have been developed for the determination of micro-constants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications_and_significance">Applications and significance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Acid_dissociation_constant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Applications and significance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A knowledge of p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values is important for the quantitative treatment of systems involving acid–base equilibria in solution. Many applications exist in <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a>; for example, the p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values of proteins and <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acid</a> side chains are of major importance for the activity of enzymes and the stability of proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[59]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Protein_pKa_calculations" title="Protein pKa calculations">Protein p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values</a> cannot always be measured directly, but may be calculated using theoretical methods. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buffer_solutions" title="Buffer solutions">Buffer solutions</a> are used extensively to provide solutions at or near the physiological pH for the study of biochemical reactions;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[60]</a></sup> the design of these solutions depends on a knowledge of the p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values of their components. Important buffer solutions include <a href="/wiki/MOPS" title="MOPS">MOPS</a>, which provides a solution with pH 7.2, and <a href="/wiki/Tricine" title="Tricine">tricine</a>, which is used in <a href="/wiki/Gel_electrophoresis" title="Gel electrophoresis">gel electrophoresis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[62]</a></sup> Buffering is an essential part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acid_base_physiology" title="Acid base physiology">acid base physiology</a> including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acid-base_homeostasis" title="Acid-base homeostasis">acid–base homeostasis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[63]</a></sup> and is key to understanding disorders such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acid-base_imbalance" title="Acid-base imbalance">acid–base imbalance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[66]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Isoelectric_point" title="Isoelectric point">isoelectric point</a> of a given molecule is a function of its p<i>K</i> values, so different molecules have different isoelectric points. This permits a technique called <a href="/wiki/Isoelectric_focusing" title="Isoelectric focusing">isoelectric focusing</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[67]</a></sup> which is used for separation of proteins by <a href="/wiki/Two-dimensional_gel_electrophoresis" title="Two-dimensional gel electrophoresis">2-D gel polyacrylamide gel electrophoresis</a>.
</p><p>Buffer solutions also play a key role in <a href="/wiki/Analytical_chemistry" title="Analytical chemistry">analytical chemistry</a>. They are used whenever there is a need to fix the pH of a solution at a particular value. Compared with an aqueous solution, the pH of a buffer solution is relatively insensitive to the addition of a small amount of strong acid or strong base. The buffer capacity<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[68]</a></sup> of a simple buffer solution is largest when pH = p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub>. In <a href="/wiki/Acid-base_extraction" title="Acid-base extraction">acid–base extraction</a>, the efficiency of extraction of a compound into an organic phase, such as an <a href="/wiki/Ether" title="Ether">ether</a>, can be optimised by adjusting the pH of the aqueous phase using an appropriate buffer. At the optimum pH, the concentration of the electrically neutral species is maximised; such a species is more soluble in organic solvents having a low <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dielectric_constant" title="Dielectric constant">dielectric constant</a> than it is in water. This technique is used for the purification of weak acids and bases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/PH_indicator" title="PH indicator">pH indicator</a> is a weak acid or weak base that changes colour in the transition pH range, which is approximately p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> ± 1. The design of a <a href="/wiki/Universal_indicator" title="Universal indicator">universal indicator</a> requires a mixture of indicators whose adjacent p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values differ by about two, so that their transition pH ranges just overlap.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Pharmacology" title="Pharmacology">pharmacology</a>, ionization of a compound alters its physical behaviour and macro properties such as solubility and <a href="/wiki/Partition_coefficient" title="Partition coefficient">lipophilicity</a>, log <i>p</i>). For example, ionization of any compound will increase the solubility in water, but decrease the lipophilicity. This is exploited in <a href="/wiki/Drug_development" title="Drug development">drug development</a> to increase the concentration of a compound in the blood by adjusting the p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> of an ionizable group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-avdeef_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-avdeef-74">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Knowledge of p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values is important for the understanding of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complex_(chemistry)" title="Complex (chemistry)">coordination complexes</a>, which are formed by the interaction of a metal ion, M<sup>m+</sup>, acting as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_acid" title="Lewis acid">Lewis acid</a>, with a <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligand</a>, L, acting as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_base" title="Lewis base">Lewis base</a>. However, the ligand may also undergo protonation reactions, so the formation of a complex in aqueous solution could be represented symbolically by the reaction
</p>
<dl><dd>[M(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub><i>n</i></sub>]<sup><i>m</i>+</sup> + LH ⇌ [M(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub><i>n</i>−1</sub>L]<sup>(<i>m</i>−1)+</sup> + H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup></dd></dl>
<p>To determine the equilibrium constant for this reaction, in which the ligand loses a proton, the p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> of the protonated ligand must be known. In practice, the ligand may be polyprotic; for example <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EDTA" title="EDTA">EDTA</a><sup>4−</sup> can accept four protons; in that case, all p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values must be known. In addition, the metal ion is subject to <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis#Hydrolysis_of_metal_aqua_ions" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a>, that is, it behaves as a weak acid, so the p<i>K</i> values for the hydrolysis reactions must also be known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Assessing the <a href="/wiki/Risk_assessment" title="Risk assessment">hazard</a> associated with an acid or base may require a knowledge of p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[72]</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_cyanide" title="Hydrogen cyanide">hydrogen cyanide</a> is a very toxic gas, because the <a href="/wiki/Cyanide#Toxicity" title="Cyanide">cyanide ion</a> inhibits the iron-containing enzyme <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome_c_oxidase" title="Cytochrome c oxidase">cytochrome c oxidase</a>. Hydrogen cyanide is a weak acid in aqueous solution with a p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> of about 9. In strongly alkaline solutions, above pH 11, say, it follows that sodium cyanide is "fully dissociated" so the hazard due to the hydrogen cyanide gas is much reduced. An acidic solution, on the other hand, is very hazardous because all the cyanide is in its acid form. Ingestion of cyanide by mouth is potentially fatal, independently of pH, because of the reaction with cytochrome c oxidase.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Environmental_science" title="Environmental science">environmental science</a> acid–base equilibria are important for lakes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[73]</a></sup> and rivers;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stumm_morgan_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-stumm_morgan-78">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aquatic_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-aquatic-79">[75]</a></sup> for example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humic_acid" title="Humic acid">humic acids</a> are important components of natural waters. Another example occurs in <a href="/wiki/Chemical_oceanography" title="Chemical oceanography">chemical oceanography</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[76]</a></sup> in order to quantify the solubility of iron(III) in seawater at various <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinities</a>, the p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values for the formation of the iron(III) hydrolysis products Fe(OH)<sup>2+</sup>, <span class="chemf nowrap">Fe(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> and Fe(OH)<sub>3</sub> were determined, along with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solubility_product" title="Solubility product">solubility product</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_hydroxide" title="Iron hydroxide">iron hydroxide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Values_for_common_substances">Values for common substances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Acid_dissociation_constant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Values for common substances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are multiple techniques to determine the p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> of a chemical, leading to some discrepancies between different sources. Well measured values are typically within 0.1 units of each other. Data presented here were taken at 25 °C in water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SA_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-SA-9">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lange_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lange-82">[78]</a></sup> More values can be found in <a href="#Thermodynamics">thermodynamics</a>, above.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Acid_dissociation_constant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Acidosis" title="Acidosis">Acidosis</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Acids_in_wine" title="Acids in wine">Acids in wine</a>: <a href="/wiki/Tartaric_acid" title="Tartaric acid">tartaric</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malic_acid" title="Malic acid">malic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid" title="Citric acid">citric</a> are the principal acids in wine.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alkalosis" title="Alkalosis">Alkalosis</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arterial_blood_gas" title="Arterial blood gas">Arterial blood gas</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chemical_equilibrium" title="Chemical equilibrium">Chemical equilibrium</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Conductivity_(electrolytic)" title="Conductivity (electrolytic)">Conductivity (electrolytic)</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Grotthuss_mechanism" title="Grotthuss mechanism">Grotthuss mechanism</a>: how protons are transferred between hydronium ions and water molecules, accounting for the exceptionally high ionic mobility of the proton (animation).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hammett_acidity_function" title="Hammett acidity function">Hammett acidity function</a>: a measure of acidity that is used for very concentrated solutions of strong acids, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superacids" title="Superacids">superacids</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ion_transport_number" title="Ion transport number">Ion transport number</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">Ocean acidification</a>: dissolution of atmospheric carbon dioxide affects <a href="/wiki/PH#Seawater" title="PH">seawater pH</a>. The reaction depends on <a href="/wiki/Total_inorganic_carbon" title="Total inorganic carbon">total inorganic carbon</a> and on solubility equilibria with solid carbonates such as <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dolomite" title="Dolomite">dolomite</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Law_of_dilution" title="Law of dilution">Law of dilution</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/PCO2" title="PCO2">pCO2</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Predominance_diagram" title="Predominance diagram">Predominance diagram</a>: relates to equilibria involving <a href="/wiki/Oxyanion" title="Oxyanion">polyoxyanions</a>. p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> values are needed to construct these diagrams.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Proton_affinity" title="Proton affinity">Proton affinity</a>: a measure of basicity in the gas phase.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Stability_constants_of_complexes" title="Stability constants of complexes">Stability constants of complexes</a>: formation of a complex can often be seen as a competition between proton and metal ion for a ligand, which is the product of dissociation of an acid.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Acid_dissociation_constant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
</div>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some chemists maintain that a dissociation occurs when two or more ionic species separate from one another (such as occurs during dissolution of an ionic solid) but that the formation of one or more ions is properly an ionization process.  Thus, sodium chloride solid dissociates in water whereas hydrogen chloride gas ionizes in water as the former already has an ionic structure whereas the later is a <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecular substance</a> with a covalent bond between the hydrogen and chlorine atoms and the gas does not consist of ions:

<dl><dd>NaCl(s)   →   Na<sup>+</sup>(aq)   +   Cl<sup>−</sup>(aq)</dd></dl>
<dl><dd>HCl(g)   + H<sub>2</sub>O   →   H<sub>3</sub>O<sup>+</sup>(aq)   +   Cl<sup>−</sup>(aq)</dd></dl>

From this perspective, <i>K</i><sub>a</sub> is actually an acid ionization constant and not an acid dissociation constant, but for most practical purposes, the terms are used interchangeably.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> is sometimes referred to as an acid dissociation constant, but this is incorrect, strictly speaking, as the constant is <i>K</i><sub>a</sub> whereas p<i>K</i><sub>a</sub> is the <a href="/wiki/Logarithm" title="Logarithm">logarithm</a> of the reciprocal of that constant.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
